        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.3c07402"><meta property="og:title" content="Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO2 Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/acsami.3c07402.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Titanium dioxide (TiO2) can serve as a candidate anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with the merits of their low cost, abundance, and environment friendliness. However, its low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and sluggish sodium-ion diffusion greatly limit its further practical applications. Herein, we report a one-step prepotassiation strategy to modify commercial TiO2 by a spontaneous chemical reaction using potassium naphthalene (K-Nt). Prepotassiation effectively compensates for the irreversible Na loss and induces a homogeneous, dense, and robust artificial solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on its surface. The well-distributed artificial SEI suppresses the excessive electrolyte decomposition, contributing to rapid interfacial kinetics and stable Na+ insertion/extraction. Therefore, such modified commercial TiO2 anodes demonstrate significantly improved ICE (72.4%) and outstanding rate performance (176.4 mAh g–1 at 5 A g–1). This simple and efficient method for promoting ICEs and interfacial chemistry also demonstrates universality and practical value for other anodes in SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/acsami.3c07402.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318833b592a54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318833b592a54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '2e13a0f6-c960-4137-a8e0-eda3cabdf93f'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/lazy-imports-6922fe6a4ca6b2e0cd9a.js"></script><script type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318833b592a54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318833b592a54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Significantly Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07402", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">7</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>) can serve as a candidate anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with the merits of their low cost, abundance, and environment friendliness. However, its low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and sluggish sodium-ion diffusion greatly limit its further practical applications. Herein, we report a one-step prepotassiation strategy to modify commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> by a spontaneous chemical reaction using potassium naphthalene (K-Nt). Prepotassiation effectively compensates for the irreversible Na loss and induces a homogeneous, dense, and robust artificial solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on its surface. The well-distributed artificial SEI suppresses the excessive electrolyte decomposition, contributing to rapid interfacial kinetics and stable Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Therefore, such modified commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes demonstrate significantly improved ICE (72.4%) and outstanding rate performance (176.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>). This simple and efficient method for promoting ICEs and interfacial chemistry also demonstrates universality and practical value for other anodes in SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D40508%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DQi%2BWang%252C%2BZhi%2BTang%252C%2BRui%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D34%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c07402%26title%3DSignificantly%2BImproving%2Bthe%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%2Bof%2BTiO2%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D40518%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes"> Electrolytes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers"> Layers </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides"> Oxides </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292274&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Surface chemistry"> Surface chemistry </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Titanium+Dioxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_titanium%20dioxide">titanium dioxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Initial+Coulombic+Efficiency&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_initial%20Coulombic%20efficiency">initial Coulombic efficiency</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Artificial+SEI+Films&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_artificial%20SEI%20films">artificial SEI films</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High-rate+Performance&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high-rate%20performance">high-rate performance</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have triggered considerable attention and developed into one competitive candidate for large-scale energy storage owing to the cost advantages and abundant sodium resources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> To search for advanced electrode materials that can effectively and reversibly accommodate large Na ions remains a big challenge, as the radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ion (1.02 Å) is significantly larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup> ion (0.76 Å). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> Among numerous anode candidates, TiO<sub>2</sub> materials are considered as attractive anodes for SIBs due to the advantages of high theoretical capacity (335 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), abundant resources, and negligible structural strain. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> However, the unsatisfactory rate capability induced by the poor intrinsic conductivity and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics has seriously restricted the development of high-performance TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To date, considerable effort has been devoted to improving the electrochemical performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes, including (i) enhancing electronic conductivity via mixing with conductive substrates; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> (ii) shortening Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion distance through making nanostructures; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> and (iii) facilitating rapid Na<sup>+</sup> migration by introducing heteroatoms. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> Despite the greatly improved electrochemical properties achieved by these strategies, the relatively low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) still exists in TiO<sub>2</sub>. For example, in our previous studies, plasma-assisted S-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> exhibited superfast sodium insertion/extraction capabilities and distinguished cycling stability but suffered from a low ICE of less than 50%. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Similarly, Xiong et al. demonstrated that N/S-codoped anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> can simultaneously achieve high electronic conductivity and stepped-up Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics; however, the ICE of NS-TiO<sub>2</sub> is only 45.3%. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> To the best of our knowledge, the ICE of most reported TiO<sub>2</sub>-based materials did not exceed 60%, meaning that a large number of Na<sup>+</sup> is irreversibly consumed from the cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> As a critical parameter, the anode with a low ICE seriously impedes the improvement of capacity, energy density, and cycle stability of SIBs. Given the low cutoff voltage settings (usually set as 0.01 V), it inevitably results in the decomposition of electrolytes and triggers the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the surface of the anode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> Moreover, the sodiation of TiO<sub>2</sub> is partially reversible during the initial sodiation process, which leads to a low ICE and cycle degradation of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes. Based on this consideration, it is critical to improve the ICE to achieve the practical application of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes in SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To improve the ICE of high-capacity anode, various strategies have been proposed including electrolyte optimization, structure regulation, and anode presodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 20 to 24">(20−24)</a> Recently, presodiation has been considered as the most powerful means to improve the ICE of high-capacity anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 20, 25 and 26">(20,25,26)</a> A simple presodiation method is contact presodiation through contacting the Na source with the anode in the working electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> However, the uncontrollable reaction extent and inhomogeneous presodiation within the electrode should be considered when applying this technique. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> In addition, highly active Na possibly creates safety concerns during battery manufacture. The external electrochemical presodiation is implemented via an electrochemical reaction between the anode and the Na metal in a half-cell; the anode could be sodiated to an ideal level via adjusting the discharge depth, but it constitutes a supererogatory step that interrupts cell fabrication. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> In contrast, the chemical presodiation appears to be a more promising strategy based on the chemical reduction reaction between the sodium-containing solution with the electrode and is widely considered as the potential to be implemented in industrialization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> However, one severe obstacle for these presodiated anodes is the uncontrollable SEI growth after immersion in the electrolyte. These unstable and unevenly distributed prefabricated SEI seriously impedes stable and fast sodium storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> To conquer these issues, it is essential to develop a method to simultaneously improve ICE and form a uniform artificial SEI.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this regard, we successfully present an ultrafast, safe, and controllable strategy to simultaneously promote ICE and construct an inorganic-rich SEI layer on the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode. Notably, such an inorganic SEI layer can be spontaneously formed on the surface of the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode when immersed into a liquid solution consisting of metal K, naphthalene (Nt), and 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME). Due to the large potential differences between K-Nt and TiO<sub>2</sub>, a spontaneous redox reaction occurs, and the artificial SEI formed on the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode (denoted KTO) is more homogeneous, dense, and thin than those generated on the pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> anode through repeated cycles (denoted TO). These KTO anodes with engineered SEI demonstrate superb ICE and remarkable rate capability. Consequently, this work provides a new strategy to both improve ICE and modulate interfacial chemistry and is expected to navigate the direction for the practical application of high-performance TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Naphthalene, 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME, C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>10</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 99%), and commercial anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> were obtained from Macklin Inc. and used directly without further purification.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Preparation of KTO Electrodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">TO electrode was fabricated by mixing the commercial anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, Super P, and poly(vinylidene difluoride) (PVDF) with a weight ratio of 7:2:1 in the NMP solvent, and the obtained slurry was coated on Cu foils. After drying in a vacuum oven at 80 °C for 12 h, the electrode plate was punched into discs of 12 mm for use. The active material loading was about 1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. For the preparation of KTO electrodes, first, metal K was added to a solution of naphthalene (Nt) in DME to form a 1 M K-Nt-DME solution. Then, the prepotassiation was conducted via immersing the TO electrode into a K-Nt-DME solution for some time at room temperature, followed by rinsing with DME solvent. For comparison, the presodiation was performed by the same steps but with the addition of Na metal during the synthesis process (denoted NTO). These operations were conducted in an argon-filled glovebox.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were collected on an X-ray diffractometer (BrukerAXS) with Cu–Kα radiation. Raman spectra were measured on a Renishaw in Via spectrometer. The morphological structures of the electrodes were characterized by the transmission electronic microscope (TEM) (JEOL JEM-F200) and field-emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM)(JEOL/JSM-7610FPlus). The surface components were investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) using a Thermo Kalpha spectrometer. The cells were disassembled in a glovebox after 5 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and the electrodes were washed several times with the DME solvent. After drying, the electrodes were transferred to the XPS chamber. The sample was etched using 2000 eV Ar ions for the depth-profiling investigation. Inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES) was conducted on an Agilent 5110 system to quantify the element content.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For the sodium-ion battery, the 2016-coin cell was assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with an O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O context below 1 ppm. Glass fiber and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME were used as the separator and electrolyte, respectively. The CR2016 coin-type cells were assembled with sodium metal as the counter/reference electrode. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were carried out on a CHI602E electrochemical workstation (Chenhua, Shanghai) at room temperature. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were measured using a Neware CT-3008W battery testing system (0.01–3.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The manufacturing schematic diagram of the KTO electrodes is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. Specifically, because of the strong electron affinity of Nt, the outer electrons of the K metals can be transferred spontaneously to the p-orbital of Nt, resulting in the formation of the Nt<sup>–</sup> radical anion (formula 1). Subsequently, when soaked in a K-Nt solution, the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode undergoes a chemical K<sup>+</sup> preinsertion reaction, driven by the strong reducing ability of the K-Nt reagent (formula 2). Once the K<sup>+</sup> is inserted, its low potential triggers the complex redox and polymerization of K<sup>+</sup> and DME solvent, forming ethereal-oligomers, ether potassium salts, and carbonate species, such as alkyl carbonates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Meanwhile, inorganic KF species are produced by redox reactions between metal K and PVDF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> As a consequence, the prefabricated K-ion SEI generated from these two reactions contains both organic and inorganic components (formulas 3 and 4). For comparison, the NTO electrode was also prepared by immersing the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode into 1 M of Na-Nt-DME solution. The reaction mechanism of NTO electrodes is similar to that of KTO, in addition to inserting Na<sup>+</sup>, and Na-ion SEI is also generated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the overall reaction mechanism of KTO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To optimize the reaction time, the TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes were immersed into the as-prepared K-Nt solution for different times (from 0 to 30 min), and the Na||KTO half-cells were used to compare the electrochemical behavior. The structure evolution of KTO at different times is demonstrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. XRD and Raman results indicate that only the intensity of the characteristic peak decreases, showing that the phase structure of the material does not change significantly. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, visible differences in the initial charging and discharging behaviors of these electrodes can be observed. The KTO anode exhibits reduced OCV and greatly decreased initial irreversible capacities with increasing immersion time. The corresponding discharge capacities are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. It is noteworthy that a 1 min reaction has helped the KTO anode reach an ICE of 72.4%, and the initial irreversible capacities cannot be completely eliminated with the increased time. This phenomenon may be related to the sodium storage mechanism of the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>. Passerini et al. reported the formation of metallic Ti (Ti<sup>0</sup>) and Ti<sup>3+</sup> during the initial discharge process, and they considered that the reduction from Ti<sup>4+</sup> to Ti<sup>0</sup> was fully irreversible, leading to irreversible sodium loss. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Therefore, we explored the structural evolution of the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode during the initial charge–discharge in ether and ester electrolytes. As seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, it is very clear that the (101) peak gradually vanishes during the discharge process, and the anatase phase could not be recovered when charging to 3.0 V, confirming an irreversible amorphization of TiO<sub>2</sub> upon the first discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The same phase evolution is observed in both ether- and ester-based electrolytes. So, based on the above analysis, it can be inferred that the irreversible reaction also reduces ICE and the specific capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Hence, in the following tests, the optimal pretreatment time was set to 1 min.</div><div class="NLM_p">The chemical reducing agents can be synthesized by mixing equal molar amounts of naphthalene and potassium/sodium metal in DME. In ether solvents, alkali metals can react with some aromatic hydrocarbons. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> As <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a reveals, the characteristic peak of Nt undergoes a red shift from 1380 to 1340 cm<sup>–1</sup> according to Raman spectra. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Meanwhile, after the formation of the K-Nt/Na-Nt solution, the solution color changes from transparent to dark green (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The phase structures of TO, NTO, and KTO samples were analyzed by XRD, as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, and all were consistent with anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (PDF#21–1272). It can be concluded that there is no obvious change in the anatase phase after the reduction treatment. The magnified XRD patterns reveal that the (101) peaks of KTO and NTO are shifted to a lower degree, implying an increased interlayer distance due to the insertion of K<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> The surface morphology of the three electrodes was captured by SEM. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c–e, the TO electrode displays a spherical structure with a size of 100 nm; in sharp contrast, the NTO and KTO surfaces become smooth, suggesting that their surfaces have been uniformly covered by analogous SEI. It is noteworthy that the pretreatment has a negligible effect on the electrode structure, and the prefabricated SEI film deposited on the KTO could effectively suppress the excessive consumption of electrolytes after cell assembly. The energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectroscopy mappings exhibit the well-distributed K and Na elements in the KTO and NTO, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>). In addition to the electrode surface, the element K is also found at the bottom of the KTO electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). This indicates that the reducing reagent can reach the overall electrode level through adequate contact; thus, a highly uniform prepotassiation of KTO is achieved.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Raman spectra of Na-Nt, K-Nt, and Nt in DME and (b) XRD patterns of TO, KTO, and NTO. Top-view SEM images of (c) TO, (d) NTO, and (e) KTO; (e<sub>1</sub>–e<sub>4</sub>) mapping images of Ti, O, C, and K.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The physicochemical properties of SEI on the electrode surface profoundly influence the electrochemical performance, which determines the ion transport through interphase. Therefore, XPS was carried out to investigate the features of the artificial SEI layer. The C 1s spectrum can be deconvoluted to four peaks including C–C (284.8 eV), C–O (286.3 eV), C═O (288.0 eV), and C–F (291.1 eV). Among them, the first three peaks are assigned to super P, and the C–F peak is attributed to PVDF (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The peak intensities of C–C for NTO and KTO decrease compared with that of TO, which should be ascribed to the deposition of the above analogous SEI. From the O 1s XPS spectrum of the TO electrode (top of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b), the signal at 529.5 eV should be assigned to the Ti–O bonds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> The peak intensity of the Ti–O bond is reduced in both KTO and NTO, while the peak in KTO is significantly higher than that in NTO, which could be further confirmed by the Ti 2p spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). The F 1s peak of the KTO shows that there is a new peak arising compared with TO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), which is attributed to KF derived from the redox reaction between metal K and PVDF as further supported by the K 2p spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). For NTO, a similar inorganic component of NaF is also produced (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f). The elemental content was quantified by inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES), where the content of Na in NTO is 5.1% and the content of K in KTO is 3.8%. To sum up, the above analysis results reveal that the SEI generated on KTO contains organic (RCH<sub>2</sub>OK) and inorganic (KF, K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) components. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Deconvoluted XPS spectra of NTO electrodes demonstrate that the SEI is comprised of similar inorganic (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NaF) and organic (RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa) species. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> Nevertheless, the major difference of the SEI layer is the relative proportion and total amount of carbon-containing byproducts, indicating that the SEI deposited on the NTO electrode is mainly dominated by Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, while the KTO primarily consists of inorganic KF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. High-resolution XPS spectra: (a) C 1s, (b) O 1s, (c) Ti 2p, (d) F 1s, (e) K 2p, and (f) Na 1s spectra of the TO, NTO, and KTO before cycling. TEM and HRTEM images of (g, j) TO, (h, k) NTO, and (i, l) KTO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To visualize the prefabricated SEI films, we further investigated the microstructural characteristics of various modified TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes using TEM. The NTO shows an uneven and thick SEI with a thickness of about 10.3 nm, while that of the KTO is only 2.9 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g–i). Therefore, it can be judged that the SEI layer produced by the NTO electrode contains more organic/inorganic components than that of the KTO electrode. Furthermore, very significant differences in the anatase (101) lattice spacing are observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>j–l), where the (101) plane distance increases to 0.365 and 0.368 nm for NTO and KTO, respectively, after presodiation/prepotassiation, indicating the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> between the TiO<sub>2</sub> layers. Subsequently, to gain an in-depth understanding of the SEI component distribution, depth-profiled XPS analysis was carried out by using Ar ion sputtering. As seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, C, O, Ti, F, and K signals on the KTO surface are identified at different etching depths. The contents of each element do not change significantly throughout the etching process, which indicates that the SEI layer in KTO exhibits good distribution with the evolution of depth (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9f</a>). It should be emphasized that the uniform artificial SEI layer is beneficial to stabilize the interfacial structure of the anode and accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, which can notably enhance the electrochemical performance of the KTO anode in SIB.</div><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the superiority of the artificial SEI, the Na half-cells were assembled, and their electrochemical performances were evaluated. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a displays the CV curves for the first two cycles in TO and KTO. An irreversible broad cathodic peak at about 0.5 V is generated during the first cycle and vanishes in the following cycles, which is ascribed to the formation of the native SEI layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> Quite differently, the peak at 0.5 V vanishes in the CV curve of the KTO anode, confirming that the artificial SEI layer was produced on the surface of the KTO anode. Besides, the CV peaks on the KTO anode became narrower and overlapped almost completely in subsequent cycles, suggesting a faster and more stable Na<sup>+</sup> insertion process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Consistent with CV results, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, the two electrodes demonstrate different initial discharge/charge behaviors. The initial discharge–charge capacities of the TO electrode are 402.9/227.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, with a corresponding ICE of 56.5%. Such a low ICE is generally caused by the electrolyte decomposition, SEI formation, and irreversible intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> in the TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Benefiting from the artificial SEI, the ICE of the KTO anode was significantly increased to 72.4% (358.4/259.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). To the best of our knowledge, the reported ICE of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes in most references for sodium storage is less than 60%. Although a high ICE of 76.6% is achieved for NTO after presodiation (288.8/221.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), the electrode exhibited a significant capacity decay (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). In comparison, the ICE of KTO is considerably higher than other reported TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anodes in SIBs, including carbon-modified TiO<sub>2</sub>, heteroatom-doped TiO<sub>2</sub>, etc. (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref16 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref16 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 3, 6 to 14, 16 and 43 to 46">(3,6−14,16,43−46)</a> It can be deduced that the uniform artificial SEI film prevents the organic electrolyte from further decomposition, and K<sup>+</sup> preinsertion into KTO enhances the diffusion coefficient and electronic conductivity, which greatly improves the electrochemical properties of the KTO anode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d, KTO keeps a steady reversible capacity of 229.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, while those of TO and NTO are 203.4 and 192.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When at a higher rate of 2A g<sup>–1</sup>, the KTO also displays excellent long-term cycling stability, with a stable capacity of 173.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 1000 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) CV curves of the TO and KTO at 0.02 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Initial charge–discharge profiles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Comparison of the ICE of the KTO electrode with previously reported TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes in SIBs. (d) Cycling performance at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Rate performance of TO, NTO, and KTO samples. (f) Charge and discharge curves of KTO at various rates. (g) Cycling performance at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (the first five cycles were performed at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate performances of these three anodes were further evaluated by increasing from 0.05 to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e). The KTO electrode demonstrates superior rate capability with 257.9, 236.6, 222.4, 209.5, 198.9, and 189.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> as the rates ranging from 50 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and a considerable capacity of 176.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is maintained even at 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, the TO and NTO electrodes deliver relatively low capacities of only 139.6 and 107.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Such a remarkable rate performance is closely related to the well-engineered SEI layer on the KTO electrode that significantly heightens Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetic, thus enabling greatly improved high-rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> Additionally, the charge/discharge profiles of the KTO electrode are well preserved at various current densities, while those for TO and NTO are almost destroyed, further demonstrating the stable phase evolution of KTO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> As displayed by the EIS results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>), the KTO anode exhibits a relatively low value and rapid stabilization of interfacial impedance compared with TO and NTO, indicating a continuously stable SEI layer on KTO. The superlative ICE and rate performance of KTO are ascribed to the stable electrode/electrolyte interphase and enhanced diffusion kinetics.</div><div class="NLM_p">The CV and GITT tests were utilized to acquire more basic insight into the rapid dynamics. As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S13</a>, well-preserved CV shapes are observed when the scan rate increases from 0.2 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, which also indicates that KTO has mild polarization and better reversibility. The polarization curves further verify that the KTO possesses an obviously lower polarization than that of the TO and NTO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). The peak separation of cathodic/anodic is only 0.083 at 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, while that for TO and NTO reaches 0.365 and 0.232 V, respectively. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics were further investigated by the GITT, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S14</a> exhibit the GITT potential profiles (the detailed calculation steps are shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Notably, the KTO electrode shows a lower overpotential and a higher <i>D</i>-value than those of TO and NTO, demonstrating significantly improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. CV curves of (a) KTO and (b) TO at different scan rates from 0.2 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (c) polarization curves, and (d) GITT potential profile of KTO. Diffusion coefficients of KTO, NTO, and TO for (e) sodiation and (f) desodiation. Temperature-dependent Nyquist plots of (g) KTO and (h) TO. (i) Arrhenius plot and calculated activation energy, <i>E</i><sub>a,ct</sub>, for three electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The interfacial charge-transfer mechanism of KTO is further investigated by temperature-dependent EIS. On the basis of Barsoukov’s model, Na<sup>+</sup> first diffuses through the electrolyte bulk and SEI film, then the charge-transfer process occurs at the SEI/electrode interface, and finally diffusion within the active material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> The EIS curves for KTO and TO anodes after five cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>g,h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S15</a>. The Nyquist plots are fitted with an equivalent circuit, and two semicycles are found in high and medium frequencies for three electrodes, indicating similar electrochemical processes in TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> However, the total impedance of the TO and NTO electrodes increases sharply when the temperature is reduced to 0 °C, while that of the KTO electrode increases slightly. The activation energy is calculated using the Arrhenius equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref40" aria-label="reference 5 and 40">(5,40)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" style="text-align: left;"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0" data-mathml="<math xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML&quot;
