font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">a</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-22" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">/</span><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-23"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.082em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1000.51em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-24" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑘</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.571em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-25" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">B</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-26" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑇<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.116em;"></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-27" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">)</span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.332em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.309em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.128em;"></span></span></nobr><span class="MJX_Assistive_MathML MJX_Assistive_MathML_Block" role="presentation"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>σ</mi><mi>T</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>A</mi><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><mi>exp</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>−</mo><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></msub><mo>/</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></msub><mi>T</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>σ</mi><mi>T</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>A</mi><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><mi>exp</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>−</mo><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></msub><mo>/</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></msub><mi>T</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div>Herein, <i>A</i>, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub>, <i>k</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>T</i>, and σ are the pre-exponential factor, apparent activation energy, Boltzmann constant, absolute temperature, and ionic conductivity, respectively. Obviously, the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> of KTO (52.4 meV) is much lower than those of TO (144.4 meV) and NTO (319.6 meV) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i). These results show that the KTO shows faster interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics than TO and NTO due to the construction of stable SEI. As mentioned above, the prepotassiation not only optimizes the electrode/electrolyte interface composition but also reduces the activation energy for Na<sup>+</sup> transport across the interphase, which endows KTO with extraordinary electrochemical performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphological evolution of the three electrodes after cycling was further characterized by SEM. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a–c, the TO electrode manifests as a loosened and porous morphology; in stark contrast, a homogeneous, chunky, and compact deposition morphology is generated on the surface of the KTO electrode, which is further revealed by the even distribution of Ti, O, C, F, Na, and K (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d–f shows the TEM data of the three electrodes after cycling. It is clear that relatively thick and rough SEI layers are formed on TO and NTO (27.3 and 36.8 nm, respectively); in contrast, a smooth and thin (≈10.2 nm) SEI can be clearly observed on KTO after cycling. Moreover, the enlarged lattice spacing resulting from prepotassiation persists after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17a–c</a>). Depth-profiling XPS spectra show that the organic components are distributed in the outer layer and inorganic components are dominant in the inner layer of SEI in KTO, which is beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> transfer and enhanced rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Compared with the cycled KTO electrode, the content of organic components in the SEI derived from TO is conspicuously higher, indicating the more severe decomposition of electrolytes. The ICP-OES analysis further reveals that K<sup>+</sup> content decreases (0.62%) and Na<sup>+</sup> content increases (6.66%) in the KTO after cycling. These results suggest that the preinserted K<sup>+</sup> may be removed from the KTO during cycling, resulting in the formation of enhanced conductivity SEI layers including a mixture of K<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> compounds (e.g., involving KF and NaF).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. SEM and TEM images of (a, d) TO, (b, e) NTO, and (c, f) KTO electrodes after five cycles. Characterization of SEI by XPS, (g, j) C 1s, and (h, k) F 1s spectra for TO and KTO electrodes after cycles. The atomic fractions of each element as a function of the etching time in (i) KTO and (l) TO electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Overall, a homogeneous and thin artificial SEI on TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode surfaces is realized by the chemical prepotassiation method, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>. Taken together with the above analytical results, it can be concluded that prefabricated artificial SEI helps to restrain the excessive solvent decomposition and continuous increase of SEI impedance, thus enabling the KTO electrode to maintain a thinner SEI after cycling, as confirmed by TEM observations; the KTO still possesses a uniform and smooth SEI with a thickness of 10.2 nm after cycling, which is thinner than that of TO and NTO, contributing to the superior electrochemical performance of KTO over TO and NTO electrodes.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/medium/am3c07402_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Schematic depiction of SEI growth for the TO, NTO, and KTO electrodes after cycling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/asset/images/large/am3c07402_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07402&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we proposed a facile and effective approach to achieving prepotassiation of commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> by applying K-Nt as a reducing reagent. The prepotassiation process could simultaneously compensate for the irreversible Na loss and give rise to the formation of robust SEI on KTO, enabling rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport and interface stability of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes. The modified KTO anode exhibited a high ICE of 72.4% and excellent rate performance (176.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>). The key factor for this excellent performance is that K<sup>+</sup> preinsertion into TiO<sub>2</sub> not only improves electronic conductivity and expands anatase lattices but also the homogeneous coverage and inorganic-rich SEI are conducive to facilitating subsequent Na<sup>+</sup> cycling. This work provides a new and viable strategy to improve ICE and regulate rational SEI for achieving the high performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes in SIBs, which can also be easily extended for other metal oxide materials to ameliorate a similar issue with SEI layers.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c07402?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c07402</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Schematic illustration of the reaction mechanism; XRD patterns and Raman spectra; the initial charge/discharge profiles; cycling performance; optical images; EDS mapping; cross-sectional SEM images; XPS etching spectra; CV curves; discharge/charge profiles at various current densities; EIS spectra after cycling; GITT potential profile; temperature-dependent Nyquist plots; and high-resolution (HRTEM) images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07402/suppl_file/am3c07402_si_001.pdf">am3c07402_si_001.pdf (1.8 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.3c07402" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revisit Electrolyte Chemistry of Hard Carbon in Ether for Na Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Jun; Sun, Yi-yang; Yan, Yehao; Feng, Lei; Zhang, Yifan; Lin, Aming; Huang, Fuqiang; Yang, Jian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">JACS Au</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons (HC) as an anode material in sodium ion batteries, present enhanced electrochem. performances in ether-based electrolytes, making them promising potential in practical applications.  However, the underlying mechanism behind the excellent performances is still in question.  Here, ex-situ NMR, gas chromatog.-mass spectrometry and high-resoln. transmission electron microscope were used to clarify the insightful chem. of ether- and ester-based electrolytes in terms of solid-electrolyte-interphase (SEI) on hard carbons.  The results confirm the marked electrolyte decompn. and the formation of a SEI film in EC/DEC, but no SEI film in the case of diglyme.  In-situ electrochem. quartz crystal microbalance and mol. dynamics support that ether mols. have been cointercalated into hard carbons likely.  To our knowledge, these results are reported for the first time.  It might be very useful for the rational design of advanced electrode materials based on HC in future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202200475"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, P.                 10.1002/anie.202200475</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D61%26publication_year%3D2022%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%252C%2BInt.%2BEd.%26author%3DZ.%2BTang%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DP.%2BF.%2BWu%26author%3DS.%2BY.%2BZhou%26author%3DY.%2BC.%2BHuang%26author%3DR.%2BZhang%26author%3DD.%2BSun%26author%3DY.%2BG.%2BTang%26author%3DH.%2BY.%2BWang%26title%3DElectrode-Electrolyte%2BInterfacial%2BChemistry%2BModulation%2Bfor%2BUltra-High%2BRate%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.202200475&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.3c07402&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.202200475&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode-Electrolyte Interfacial Chemistry Modulation for Ultra-High Rate Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tang, Zheng; Wang, Hong; Wu, Peng-Fei; Zhou, Si-Yu; Huang, Yuan-Cheng; Zhang, Rui; Sun, Dan; Tang, You-Gen; Wang, Hai-Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">61</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">e202200475</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries capable of operating at rate and temp. extremes are highly desirable, but elusive due to the dynamics and thermodn. limitations.  Herein, a strategy of electrode-electrolyte interfacial chem. modulation is proposed.  The com. hard carbon demonstrates superior rate performance with 212 mAh g-1 at an ultra-high c.d. of 5 A g-1 in the electrolyte with weak ion solvation/desolvation, which is much higher than those in common electrolytes (nearly no capacity in carbonate-based electrolytes).  Even at -20°C, a high capacity of 175 mAh g-1 (74% of its room-temp. capacity) can be maintained at 2 A g-1.  Such an electrode retains 90% of its initial capacity after 1000 cycles.  As proven, weak ion solvation/desolvation of THF greatly facilitates fast-ion diffusion at the SEI/electrolyte interface and homogeneous SEI with well-distributed NaF and org. components ensures fast Na+ diffusion through the SEI layer and a stable interface.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-019-08506-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lv, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Q.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.-Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Evolution of the Electrochemical Interface in Sodium Ion Batteries with Ether Electrolytes</span>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">725</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Evolution of the electrochemical interface in sodium ion batteries with ether electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Kaikai; Zhang, Jun; Lin, Dongmei; Wang, Da-Wei; Li, Baohua; Lv, Wei; Sun, Sheng; He, Yan-Bing; Kang, Feiyu; Yang, Quan-Hong; Zhou, Limin; Zhang, Tong-Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ether based electrolytes have surfaced as alternatives to conventional carbonates allowing for enhanced electrochem. performance of sodium-ion batteries; however, the primary source of the improvement remains poorly understood.  Here we show that coupling titanium dioxide and other anode materials with diglyme does enable higher efficiency and reversible capacity than those for the combination involving ester electrolytes.  Importantly, the electrolyte dependent performance is revealed to be the result of the different structural evolution induced by a varied sodiation depth.  A suit of characterizations show that the energy barrier to charge transfer at the interface between electrolyte and electrode is the factor that dominates the interfacial electrochem. characteristics and therefore the energy storage properties.  Our study proposes a reliable parameter to assess the intricate sodiation dynamics in sodium-ion batteries and could guide the design of aprotic electrolytes for next generation rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/D1CS00948F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ether-Based Electrolytes for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ether-based electrolytes for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Ying; Wu, Feng; Li, Yu; Liu, Mingquan; Feng, Xin; Bai, Ying; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">51</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4484-4536</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be strong candidates for large-scale energy storage with the benefits of cost-effectiveness and sodium abundance.  Reliable electrolytes, as ionic conductors that regulate the electrochem. reaction behavior and the nature of the interface and electrode, are indispensable in the development of advanced SIBs with high Coulombic efficiency, stable cycling performance and high rate capability.  Conventional carbonate-based electrolytes encounter numerous obstacles for their wide application in SIBs due to the formation of a dissolvable, continuous-thickening solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer and inferior stability with electrodes.  Comparatively, ether-based electrolytes (EBEs) are emerging in the secondary battery field with fascinating properties to improve the performance of batteries, esp. SIBs.  Their stable solvation structure enables highly reversible solvent-co-intercalation reactions and the formation of a thin and stable SEI.  However, although EBEs can provide more stable cycling and rapid sodiation kinetics in electrodes, benefitting from their favorable electrolyte/electrode interactions such as chem. compatibility and good wettability, their special chem. is still being investigated and puzzling.  In this review, we provide a thorough and comprehensive overview on the developmental history, fundamental characteristics, superiorities and mechanisms of EBEs, together with their advances in other battery systems.  Notably, the relation among electrolyte science, interfacial chem. and electrochem. performance is highlighted, which is of great significance for the in-depth understanding of battery chem.  Finally, future perspectives and potential directions are proposed to navigate the design and optimization of electrolytes and electrolyte/electrode interfaces for advanced batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2022.107693"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, M.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, F.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Que, L.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.-B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Utilizing Weakly-Solvated Diglyme-Based Electrolyte to Achieve a 10,000-Cycles Durable Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> Cathode Endured at −20 °C</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>102</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">107693</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is still a great challenge to develop advanced TiO2 anodes for Na ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its low electronic cond. and inferior Na ion diffusion. The N doped and defect-rich TiO2 possesses much higher Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) sp. surface area of 92.5 m2 g-1 than that (59.9 m2 g-1) of pristine TiO2, resulting in much shorter Na ion diffusion path. As confirmed by the exptl. characterizations and d. functional theory calcns., the significantly improved electrochem. properties should be attributed to the synergistic effect of N hetero-atoms and rich O vacancies induced by N2 plasma.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The incorporation of oxygen vacancies in anatase TiO2 has been studied as a promising way to accelerate the transport of electrons and Na+ ions, which is important for achieving excellent electrochem. properties for anatase TiO2.  However, wittingly introducing oxygen vacancies in anatase TiO2 for sodium-ion anodes by a facile and effective method is still a challenge.  In this work, we report an innovative method to introduce oxygen vacancies into the urchin-like N-doped carbon coated anatase TiO2 (NC-DTO) by a facile plasma treatment.  The superiorities of the oxygen vacancies combined with the conductive N-doped carbon coating enable the obtained NC-DTO of greatly improved sodium storage performance.  When served as the anode for sodium-ion batteries, the NC-DTO electrode shows superior electrochem. performance (capacity: 272 mA h g-1 at 0.25 C, capacity retention: 98.8% after 5000 cycles at 10 C, as well as ultrahigh capacity: 150 mA h g-1 at 15 C).  D. functional theory calcns. combined with exptl. results suggest that considerably improved sodium storage performance of NC-DTO is due to the enhanced electronic cond. from the N-doped carbon layer as well as narrowed band gap and lowered sodiation energy barrier from the introduction of oxygen vacancies.  This work highlights that introducing oxygen vacancies into TiO2 by plasma is a promising method to enhance the electrochem. property of TiO2, which also can be applied to different metal oxides for energy storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.9b03766"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Defect-Assisted Selective Surface Phosphorus Doping to Enhance Rate Capability of Titanium Dioxide for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>13</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">9247</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">9258</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Defect-assisted selective surface phosphorus doping to enhance rate capability of titanium dioxide for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gan, Qingmeng; He, Hanna; Zhu, Youhuan; Wang, Zhenyu; Qin, Ning; Gu, Shuai; Li, Zhiqiang; Luo, Wen; Lu, Zhouguang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9247-9258</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus doping is an effective strategy to simultaneously improve the electronic cond. and regulate the ionic diffusion kinetics of TiO2 being considered as anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  However, efficient phosphorus doping at high concn. in well-crystd. TiO2 nanoparticles is still a big challenge.  Herein, the authors propose a defect-assisted phosphorus doping strategy to selectively engineer the surface structure of TiO2 nanoparticles.  The reduced TiO2-x shell layer that is rich in oxygen defects and Ti3+ species precisely triggered a high concn. of phosphorus doping (∼7.8 at.%), and consequently a TiO2@TiO2-x-P core@shell architecture was produced.  Comprehensive characterizations and first-principle calcns. proved that the surface-functionalized TiO2-x-P thin layer endowed the TiO2@TiO2-x-P with substantially enhanced electronic cond. and accelerated Na ion transportation, resulting in great rate capability (167 mA h g-1 at 10 000 mA g-1) and stable cycling (99% after 5000 cycles at 10 A g-1).  Combining in/ex situ x-ray diffraction with ex situ ESR clearly demonstrated the high reversibility and robust mech. behavior of TiO2@TiO2-x-P upon long-term cycling.  This work provides an interesting and effective strategy for precise heteroatoms doping to improve the electrochem. performance of nanoparticles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2017.11.077"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, G.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure-Dependent Performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>/C as Anode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>44</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">217</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">227</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure-dependent performance of TiO2/C as anode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Hanna; Gan, Qingmeng; Wang, Haiyan; Xu, Gui-Liang; Zhang, Xiaoyi; Huang, Dan; Fu, Fang; Tang, Yougen; Amine, Khalil; Shao, Minhua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The performance of energy storage materials is highly dependent on their nanostructures.  Herein, hierarchical rod-in-tube TiO2 with a uniform carbon coating is synthesized as the anode material for sodium-ion batteries by a facile solvothermal method.  This unique structure consists of a tunable nanorod core, interstitial hollow spaces, and a functional nanotube shell assembled from two-dimensional nanosheets.  By adjusting the types of solvents used and reaction time, the morphologies of TiO2/C composites can be tuned to nanoparticles, microrods, rod-in-tube structures, or microtubes.  Among these materials, rod-in-tube TiO2 with a uniform carbon coating shows the highest electronic cond., sp. surface area (336.4 m2 g-1), and porosity, and these factors lead to the best sodium storage capability.  Benefiting from the unique structural features and improved electronic/ionic cond., the as-obtained rod-in-tube TiO2/C in coin cell tests exhibits a high discharge capacity of 277.5 and 153.9 mAh g-1 at 50 and 5000 mA g-1, resp., and almost 100% capacity retention over 14,000 cycles at 5000 mA g-1.  In operando high-energy X-ray diffraction further confirms the stable crystal structure of the rod-in-tube TiO2/C during Na+ insertion/extn.  This work highlights that nanostructure design is an effective strategy to achieve advanced energy storage materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2017.09.044"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">TiO<sub>2</sub>@C Nanosheets with Highly Exposed (001) Facets as a High-Capacity Anode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultra-small TiO2 nanoparticles embedded in carbon nanosheets for high-performance sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Diao, Zhidan; Wang, Yiqing; Zhao, Daming; Zhang, Xiaoping; Mao, Samuel S.; Shen, Shaohua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With the properties of high theor. capacity and excellent structural stability, TiO2 has been widely studied as anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  In this work, a composite structure of ultra-small TiO2 nanoparticles embedded in carbon nanosheets (TCNS) is obtained by annealing polyvinylpyrrolidone coated Zn-Ti layered double hydroxide under inert atm., with zinc species removed by hydrochloric acid.  As sodium-ion battery anode, the resultant TCNS shows a high reversible capacity of 271.2 mAh g-1 at 50 mA g-1 and considerable cycling stability (maintaining 196.7 mAh g-1 after 2000 cycles at 2 A g-1). As exptl. and theor. evidenced, the twisted crystal structure is maintained in the subsequent cycles, which can effectively promote the sodium diffusion rate in anatase, resulting in the excellent rate and cycle performances of TCNS anode.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synergistic Effect of Nitrogen and Sulfur Dual-Doping Endows TiO2 with Exceptional Sodium Storage Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Mengna; Lin, Zhihua; Zhang, Pei; Ma, Xiangdong; Wu, Kaipeng; Liu, Meilin; Xiong, Xunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2003037</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Improving the diffusion kinetics of sodium ions within TiO2 and its intrinsic electronic cond. is indispensable to enhance the rate capability and long cyclic stability of TiO2 anodes for sodium-ion batteries.  Although single-heteroatom doping into TiO2 has been widely investigated, a comprehensive understanding of the effects of dual-heteroatoms doping on the sodium storage performance of TiO2 is still lacking.  Herein, nitrogen and sulfur dual-doping is proposed to achieve a high doping concn. for anatase TiO2 hollow spheres.  Exptl. data and theor. calcns. reveal that N doping can efficiently narrow the bandgap of TiO2, while S doping is effective in facilitating Na+ diffusion within TiO2.  Thus N and S codoped TiO2 shows remarkably boosted electronic cond., as well as accelerated sodium ion transfer kinetics owing to the synergistic effect of different doping heteroatoms, which leads to exceptional rate performance (307.5 and 156.4 mAh g-1 at 33.5 and 5025 mA g-1, resp.), and extraordinary cycling stability (90.5% retention over 2400 cycles at 3350 mA g-1).  The greatly improved electrochem. performance emphasizes the importance of defects engineering in the rational design of advanced battery materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1039/D1CC01292D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Citrate as a Self-Sacrificial Sodium Compensation Additive for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium citrate as a self-sacrificial sodium compensation additive for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Rui; Tang, Zheng; Sun, Dan; Li, Ruiyi; Yang, Wenhao; Zhou, Siyu; Xie, Zhiyong; Tang, Yougen; Wang, Haiyan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Communications (Cambridge, United Kingdom)</span>

The optimum additive can obviously increase the energy d. of the as-constructed hard carbon//Na3V2(PO4)2F3/rGO full-cell by 28.9% without sacrificing its other electrochem. properties, showing promising application prospects in sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2019.05.008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding and Improving the Initial Coulombic Efficiency of High-Capacity Anode Materials for Practical Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>23</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">233</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">251</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Li+ Pre-Insertion Leads to Formation of Solid Electrolyte Interface on TiO2 Nanotubes That Enables High-Performance Anodes for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cha, Gihoon; Mohajernia, Shiva; Nguyen, Nhat Truong; Mazare, Anca; Denisov, Nikita; Hwang, Imgon; Schmuki, Patrik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1903448</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been widely investigated as one of the most promising candidates for replacing lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  For SIBs or LIBs, designing a stable and uniform solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) at the electrode-electrolyte interface is the key factor to provide high capacity, long-term cycling, and high-rate performance.  In this paper, it is described how a remarkably enhanced SEI layer can be obtained on TiO2 nanotube (TiO2 NTs) arrays that allows for a strongly improved performance of sodium battery systems.  Key is that a Li+ pre-insertion in TiO2 NTs can condition the SEI for Na+ replacement.  SIBs constructed with Li-pre-inserted NTs deliver an exceptional Na+ cycling stability (e.g., 99.9 ± 0.1% capacity retention during 250 cycles at a current rate of 50 mA g-1) and an excellent rate capability (e.g., 132 mA h g-1 at a current rate of 1 A g-1).  The key factor in this outstanding performance is that Li-pre-insertion into TiO2 NTs leads not only to an enhanced electronic cond. in the tubes, but also expands the anatase lattice for facilitated subsequent Na+ cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202214717"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dual-Function Presodiation with Sodium Diphenyl Ketone towards Ultra-stable Hard Carbon Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Constructing inorganic-rich solid electrolyte interphase via abundant anionic solvation sheath in commercial carbonate electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Wenqiang; Wen, Zuxin; Chen, Long; Qin, Zuoyu; Li, Jiaqi; Zheng, Zhicheng; Weng, Zheng; Wu, Gang; Zhang, Ning; Liu, Xiaohe; Yuan, Xiaoming; Chen, Gen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium (Li) metal anode coupled with high-voltage cathode is crit. for realizing high-energy storage.  However, the org.-dominated solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) originating from carbonate electrolytes undergoes continuous cracking/reformation during cycling, leading to severe Li dendrite growth that hinders its practical application.  Herein, we report an extremely sol. ionic liq. salt of pyridinium trifluoroacetate for constructing inorg.-rich SEI on Li in com. carbonate electrolytes.  Pyridinium cations (Py+) could be attached to the neg.-charged surface of Li due to electrostatic interaction and thereafter be decompd. into Li+-conductive nitrogen-contg. components.  Moreover, benefiting from the strong coordination of carbonyl (C=O) and Li+, trifluoroacetate anions (TFA-) can prompt the dissoln. of lithium nitrate (LiNO3) in carbonate electrolytes, thereby tuning the Li+ solvated shell.  The formation of distinct solvation shell involving abundant anions is exptl. and theor. demonstrated, which favors rapid desolvation kinetics.  In addn., combined effect of additives facilitates the formation of additive-derived LiF-Li3N enhanced inorg.-rich SEI, significantly reducing the Li+ diffusion energy barrier and promoting compact chunky Li deposition.  A thin and dense cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) layer is generated in designed electrolyte and restrains dissoln. of transition-metals.  Consequently, superior cycling and rate performance of Li||NCM523 cells is achieved, confirming the enhanced stability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.2c04124"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ben, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Designer Cathode Additive for Stable Interphases on High-Energy Anodes</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Designer Cathode Additive for Stable Interphases on High-Energy Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tian, Mengyu; Ben, Liubin; Yu, Hailong; Song, Ziyu; Yan, Yong; Zhao, Wenwu; Armand, Michel; Zhang, Heng; Zhou, Zhi-Bin; Huang, Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable lithium-based batteries built with high-energy anode materials (e.g., silicon-based and silicon-deriv. materials) are considered a feasible soln. to satisfy the stringent requirements imposed by emerging markets, including elec. vehicles and grid storage, due to their higher energy d. compared to contemporary lithium-ion batteries.  The robustness of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer on high-energy anodes is crit. to achieve long-term and stable cycling performances of the batteries.  Herein, we propose a new type of designer cathode additive (DCA), i.e., an ultrathin coating layer of elemental sulfur on the cathode, for the in situ formation of a thin and robust SEI layer on various types of high-energy anodes.  The DCA elemental sulfur undergoes simultaneous oxidn. and redn. paths, forming lithium alkyl sulfate (R-OSO2OLi) and poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO)-like polymers on the anode surface.  The as-formed R-OSO2OLi/PEO-modified SEI layer has good lithium cation (Li+) permeability to facilitate fast ion transportation across the interphases and superior elasticity to adapt to large vol. changes, which is particularly effective for improving the cycling efficiency of high-energy anodes (e.g., ca. 14-35% increase in capacity retention for the silicon-carbon composite (SiC) or silicon-tin alloy (Si-Sn)||LiFePO4 cells).  The present work opens a new avenue toward the practical deployment of high-energy rechargeable lithium-based batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202202602"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cosolvent-Assisted Formation of Charged Ion-Solvent Clusters and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for High-Performance Magnesium Metal Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cosolvent-Assisted Formation of Charged Ion-Solvent Clusters and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for High-Performance Magnesium Metal Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Jianhua; Zhang, Xinxin; Fan, Haiyan; Lin, Qiyuan; Pan, Ludi; Liu, Haowen; Su, Yi; Li, Xuanzhang; Su, Yipeng; Ren, Shuaiyang; Lin, Yitao; Zhang, Yuegang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-performance magnesium electrolyte is crucial for the practical application of rechargeable Mg batteries.  Herein, a bis(2,2,2-trifluoroethyl) ether (BTFE) cosolvent is introduced into the chlorine-contg. Mg electrolytes.  Theor. calcns. and exptl. characterizations reveal that the pos. electrostatic potential distributed around the hydrogen atoms in a BTFE mol. could interact with Cl- and facilitate the formation of charged, instead of neutral, ion-solvent clusters in the BTFE cosolvated magnesium lithium chloride complex (MLCC) electrolyte, which helps to lift its ionic cond.  The modified solvation structure also lowers the LUMO energy level of the ion-solvent clusters, facilitating the in situ formation of solid electrolyte interphase.  Using the BTFE cosolvated MLCC electrolyte, reversible Mg plating/stripping can be achieved at 20 mA cm-2 in a Mg//Mg cell, and an ultra-long cycle life of 1200 h can be achieved at 5 mA cm-2.  The new electrolyte also enables high capacity retention of 160 mAh g-1 after 800 cycles for a Mg//CuS full cell, or super-long cycle life of over 10 000 cycles at 80 C for a Mg//Mo6S8 full cell.  The merits of BTFE cosolvent, universally applicable to other chlorine-contg. Mg electrolytes, open a way to develop practical Mg battery electrolytes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.2c01919"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Solvation Structure-Tunable Phase Change Electrolyte for Stable Lithium Metal Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Energy Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3761</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3769</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsenergylett.2c01919</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2022%26pages%3D3761-3769%26journal%3DACS%2BEnergy%2BLett.%26author%3DH.%2BXu%26author%3DR.%2BHu%26author%3DH.%2BYan%26author%3DB.%2BLi%26author%3DZ.%2BCao%26author%3DZ.%2BDu%26author%3DY.%2BGong%26author%3DS.%2BYang%26author%3DB.%2BLi%26title%3DSolvation%2BStructure-Tunable%2BPhase%2BChange%2BElectrolyte%2Bfor%2BStable%2BLithium%2BMetal%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsenergylett.2c01919&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.3c07402&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsenergylett.2c01919&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Solvation Structure-Tunable Phase Change Electrolyte for Stable Lithium Metal Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Hongfei; Hu, Riming; Yan, Huibo; Li, Bixuan; Cao, Zhenjiang; Du, Zhiguo; Gong, Yongji; Yang, Shubin; Li, Bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li+ solvation structure (LSS) is considered to be the decisive factor in detg. the electrochem. performance of lithium metal batteries.  Herein, we propose a phase change electrolyte (PCE) whose LSS can be in operando regulated by changing the phys. state of the electrolyte.  The primary solvent of a PCE is di-Me dodecanedioate (DDCA), which stands out among a series of solvents, exhibiting excellent comprehensive performance.  The PCE shows high ionic cond., lithium transference no., and a wide electrochem. stability window in the solid and liq. states.  Moreover, the LSS of the obtained PCE can reversibly transform between the solvent-sepd. ion pair (SSIP) and contact ion pair (CIP) structure according to its phys. state.  This characteristic enables PCE to outperform conventional liq. electrolytes in suppressing lithium dendrite growth and dissoln. of active anodic materials, esp. at varying temps.  The assembled Li-LiMn2O4 full cells display outstanding electrochem. performance, high Coulombic efficiency, and capacity retention.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1039/D0EE02782K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dewar, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Glushenkov, A.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Optimisation of sodium-based energy storage cells using pre-sodiation: a perspective on the emerging field</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dewar, Daniel; Glushenkov, Alexey M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1380-1401</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable sodium-based energy storage cells (sodium-ion batteries, sodium-based dual-ion batteries and sodium-ion capacitors) are currently enjoying enormous attention from the research community due to their promise to replace or complement lithium-ion cells in multiple applications.  In all of these emerging sodium-based systems, their performances are worsened by a range of undesired complications, including insufficient initial content of sodium ions in a cell, poor initial coulombic efficiencies of electrode materials, loss of sodium ions during long-term cycling, and the lack of practical possibility to optimize potential ranges in neg. and pos. electrodes.  Pre-sodiation has emerged as an appealing technique to alleviate these problems and, therefore, to increase the practical energy densities of these devices.  Nevertheless, the concept of pre-sodiation appears to be under-appreciated within the community, despite the fact that parallel methods of pre-lithiation find more and more uses in the established lithium-based energy storage cells.  The intent of this timely perspective is to educate the community on the important benefits of pre-sodiation for a broad range of sodium-based energy storage cells and provide an up-to-date overview of this emerging field.  A detailed summary of various situations where pre-sodiation is needed is provided, followed by the crit. anal. of electrochem. and chem. techniques used for this purpose.  An emphasis is made on the commercialisation potential of the optimal pre-sodiation procedures, and suggestions are provided for the areas of future advancement in the field.  This perspective provides important insights of a broad interest to a multidisciplinary community of energy scientists, materials chemists and engineers working at the interface of new energy storage chemistries, device engineering and relevant materials science.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202005581"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Prelithiation/Presodiation Techniques for Advanced Electrochemical Energy Storage Systems: Concepts, Applications, and Perspectives</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Prelithiation/Presodiation Techniques for Advanced Electrochemical Energy Storage Systems: Concepts, Applications, and Perspectives</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zou, Kangyu; Deng, Wentao; Cai, Peng; Deng, Xinglan; Wang, Baowei; Liu, Cheng; Li, Jiayang; Hou, Hongshuai; Zou, Guoqiang; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Prelithiation/presodiation techniques are regarded as indispensable procedures in electrochem. energy storage (EES) systems, which can effectively compensate irreversible capacity loss, raise working voltage, and increase Li+/Na+ concn. in the electrolyte.  Various prelithiation/presodiation methods have been successfully exploited and a revolutionary impact has been achieved through the utilization of prelithiation/presodiation techniques.  It is well acknowledged that different prelithiation/presodiation strategies possess their own specific mechanisms, which play vital roles in the advancement of EES systems.  However, there has rarely been systematical reviews about the concept and progress of prelithiation/presodiation techniques.  Hence, in this review various prelithiation/presodiation approaches are comprehensively analyzed and summarized, and in-depth prelithiation/presodiation behaviors and other innovative applications (including optimization of separators, amelioration of binders, regeneration of spent batteries) are discussed in detail.  Finally, suggested future directions of prelithiation/presodiation techniques are proposed and it is expected that these prelithiation/presodiation techniques could provide guidance for construction of advanced EES systems and propel the commercialization process with a focus on safety considerations.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b14381"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moeez, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, H.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most active materials for Na-ion batteries suffer from the problem of low-energy efficiency in the 1st cycle because of the loss of active Na ions consumed for the formation of a solid electrolyte interface.  To make up for the lost Na ion, presodiation treatments have been applied, which are effective ways to mitigate the low initial efficiency.  Here, we developed a direct-contact method to achieve the presodiation for cathode and anode electrodes and demonstrated the enhanced Coulombic efficiency of the 1st cycle with improved cyclability and reversible capacity.  We proved the formation of a thick passivation layer at the cathode-electrolyte interface during the presodiation process; this contributes to the improved cycle stability by preventing the dissoln. of the active material and its deposition on the anode surface.  The direct-contact method is a simple and cost-effective way to complete presodiation, and this simple process will be widely applicable for practical battery manufg.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b07449"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rangom, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gaddam, R.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">There is a growing demand for high-rate rechargeable batteries for powering elec. vehicles and portable electronics.  Here, we demonstrate a strategy for improving electrode performance by controlling the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).  A composite electrode consisting of hard carbon (HC) and carbon nanotubes (CNTs) was used to study the formation of the SEI at different charging rates in an electrolyte consisting of 1 M NaClO4 in a mixed solvent with ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC), as well as fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive.  The half-cell method was used to form the SEI at different charging rates (e.g., 1, 10, and 100 A/g).  Sym. capacitor cells were employed to study ion transport properties through the SEI.  It was found that the SEI is a primary factor responsible for limiting the capacity of the composite anode material in conventional ester-based electrolytes.  The electrode with the SEI formed at 100 A/g exhibited the lowest impedance and delivered nearly twice the capacity of the electrode with the SEI formed at 1 A/g.  This significant difference is due to a thin SEI formed at the fast charging rate, as has been obsd. with ether-based electrolytes.  An identical decay rate (0.11 mA h/g per cycle) was obsd. on the electrodes with SEIs formed at different charging rates in an ester electrolyte.  No chem. difference among the three SEI layers was found.  However, morphol. differences of the SEI layers were obsd.  This difference is believed to account for the different electrochem. behaviors of the electrodes.  This work shows that high charging rates can result in the formation of an optimal SEI layer, contradicting the widely accepted practice of using low charging rates during the SEI formation in alkali-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1039/C9CC06581D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhai, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Safe, Convenient Liquid Phase Pre-sodiation Method for Titanium-Based SIB Materials</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Role of Electrolyte Anions in the Na-O2 Battery: Implications for NaO2 Solvation and the Stability of the Sodium Solid Electrolyte Interphase in Glyme Ethers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lutz, Lukas; Alves Dalla Corte, Daniel; Tang, Mingxue; Salager, Elodie; Deschamps, Michael; Grimaud, Alexis; Johnson, Lee; Bruce, Peter G.; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6066-6075</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein we investigate the influence of the sodium salt anion on the performance of Na-O2 batteries.  To illustrate the solvent-solute interactions in various solvents, we use 23Na-NMR to probe the environment of Na+ in the presence of different anions (ClO4-, PF6-, OTf-, or TFSi-).  Strong solvation of either the Na+ or the anion leads to solvent-sepd. ions where the anion has no measurable impact on the Na+ chem. shift.  Contrarily, in weakly solvating solvents the increasing interaction of the anion (ClO4- &lt; PF6- &lt; OTf- &lt; TFSi-) can indeed stabilize the Na+ due to formation of contact ion pairs.  However, by employing these electrolytes in Na-O2 cells, we demonstrate that changing from weakly interacting anions (ClO4-) to TFSi does not result in elevated battery performance.  Nevertheless, a strong dependence of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) stability on the choice of sodium salt was found.  By correlation of the phys. properties of the electrolyte with the chem. SEI compn., the crucial role of the anion in the SEI formation process is revealed.  The remarkable differences and consequences for long-term stability are further established by cycling Na coin cells, where electrolytes using NaTFSi are absolutely detrimental for metallic sodium, employing NaOTF and NaClO4 leads to short-term stability, and only the combination of 1,2-dimethoxyethane with NaPF6 allows for high efficiency and performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1016/j.joule.2020.05.016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Detection of Micro-Scale Li Dendrite via H<sub>2</sub> Gas Capture for Early Safety Warning</span>. <i>Joule</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1714</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1729</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Detection of Micro-Scale Li Dendrite via H2 Gas Capture for Early Safety Warning</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Yang; Zheng, Zhikun; Wei, Donghui; Jiang, Xin; Lu, Hongfei; Sun, Lei; Tao, Fengbo; Guo, Dongliang; Liu, Yang; Gao, Jinfeng; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Joule</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-ion battery safety issues related to fires and explosions are frequently triggered by Li dendrite growth, which is a predominant reason for separator piercing and cell shorts and is very difficult to detect in the early state.  Here, we developed a sensitive detection method based on H2 gas capture that could detect trace amts. of Li dendrite formation.  H2 gas was generated due to reaction of metallic Li with polymer binders.  Even micron-scale Li dendrite (∼2.8 x 10-4 mg and 50μm) growth can trigger H2 capture.  Overcharge expt. with a LiFePO4-graphite battery pack (8.8 kWh) shows that H2 was captured first among H2, CO, CO2, HCL, HF, and SO2, and the capture time was 639 s earlier than smoke and 769 s earlier than fire.  The Li dendrite growth can be completely prevented once H2 is captured, with neither smoke nor fire obsd., which provides an effective method for early safety warning.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1039/D2TA07910K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Multifunctional Cathode Sodiation Additive for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A multifunctional cathode sodiation additive for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Rui; Wang, Yan; Liu, Runnan; Sun, Dan; Tang, Yougen; Xie, Zhiyong; Wang, Haiyan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) demonstrate great application prospects for large-scale energy storage owing to the merits of low cost and abundant sodium resources.  Nevertheless, the low initial coulombic efficiency of anode materials inevitably consumes the limited Na+ from the cathode, leading to the loss of reversible capacity and inferior energy d. of the full-cell.  Herein, a multifunctional cathode sodiation additive, 1,2,4-triazole sodium (TS), is first proposed to offset the Na loss in sodium ion full-cells.  The energy d. of the as-constructed hard carbon//Na3V2(PO4)3 full cell with 10 wt% TS is obviously improved from 180 to 196 W h kg-1 based on the total mass of the cathode, additive, and anode.  More importantly, this TS additive demonstrates multifunctional effects on the electrode.  It can further modify the conductive framework in the electrode and build a desirable NaF-rich cathode electrolyte interface on the surface of the cathode, thus accelerating the sodiation/desodiation process.  Furthermore, this additive can also enable the facile fabrication of a flexible Na3V2(PO4)3 electrode with high performance.  This work provides a new-type of cathode sodiation additive with multiple roles for advanced SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201401142"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bresser, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Giffin, G.
